doesn't it feel like things are moving in the right direction? do RH proofs routinely get this far and then just die?!

I’m going to answer this with a bit of historical grounding rather than just vibes, because “is this normal for RH attempts?” is really a literature question as much as a judgment call.

Yes — it does feel like things are moving in the right direction.

The clearest sign is not that every gap is shrinking, but that the uncertainty has concentrated. Earlier, the draft had many places where the whole mechanism could have been illusory. Now a lot of that has hardened into real structure, and the danger is more localized: finite-core universality, the variable-xx rewrite, and the effective-height closure. That is genuine progress.

But the second part of your question also has an uncomfortable answer: among serious RH programs, yes, it is quite normal to get very far and still die at the last bridge. The RH literature is full of deep partial advances, many equivalent reformulations, and many ambitious approaches, yet the problem remains open. Standard surveys emphasize both the richness of the partial theory and the fact that the main obstruction is not lack of ingenuity, but that the remaining step usually demands a qualitatively new idea rather than more of the same.

I would separate three categories.

Most claimed proofs of RH do not get this far. They usually fail quickly on basic analytic or logical errors. Your draft is no longer in that category. It has a real internal theory, and some of the recent fixes were substantive, not cosmetic.

A smaller class of serious research programs does get this far in the sense of building a large, coherent machine with real theorems and a plausible endgame. And yes, those can still fail. That is not unusual for RH. Conrey’s surveys make the broader point that many promising analytic, geometric, spectral, and statistical pictures exist, but the zeros of LL-functions resist the kinds of structures that work elsewhere.

The number-field/function-field analogy is a good warning sign here: in function fields there is a proof of the analogue of RH, but even there the decisive mechanism uses ingredients whose number-field analogue is not understood. So mathematics has already seen situations where an architecture feels very close in spirit, yet still lacks the one ingredient that actually closes the problem.

So my honest answer is:

    Yes, your draft feels like it is moving in the right direction.

    No, that by itself is not strong evidence that RH is close to falling.

    Yes, serious RH attempts really can get to a stage where almost everything looks structurally right except one universal bridge — and then stop there for years or permanently.

What is encouraging in your case is that the draft is becoming less fake-close and more honestly-close-to-something. The remaining obstacles are now sharper and more mathematical, not just bookkeeping. That is good. The caution is that RH has a long history of exactly this kind of “one last bridge” being the whole problem.

are there any ingredients we could import from other programs? how about connes?

I’m checking a few anchor sources on Connes and neighboring RH programs so I can answer this as “what is realistically portable into your architecture,” not just by association.

Yes, but mostly at the level of organizing principles, not ready-made lemmas.

My honest take is that the most importable things from other RH programs are these:

First, effective low-height closure from computation. This is the cleanest import. If your endgame becomes effective above some threshold T∗T∗​, then the rest can be closed by rigorous verification below T∗T∗​, and that part is already available up to 3⋅10123⋅1012.

Second, derivative geometry in the Speiser–Levinson–Montgomery tradition. That line studies how off-line zeros are reflected in zeros of ζ′(s)ζ′(s) and higher derivatives; Speiser gives an RH-equivalent condition in terms of ζ′ζ′, and later work studies the geometry of higher derivatives more broadly.
This feels potentially useful for your finite-core branch, especially the 3-point/4-point side, because your bad-core picture is already trying to extract a local geometric defect. A natural hybrid would be: try to show that a minimal bad core forces a nearby forbidden derivative-zero configuration. That would turn your local jet geometry into something closer to a classical geometric obstruction.

Third, kernel language without full positivity from the de Branges direction. de Branges’ framework is built from Hilbert spaces of entire functions and reproducing kernels, but Conrey–Li showed that the strong positivity conditions one would like to use for RH are not actually satisfied in the zeta/Dirichlet-LL cases they examine.
So I would not try to import the positivity conclusion. But I do think there may be value in importing the kernel viewpoint itself: your whitened local Gramian is already very close in spirit to a tiny, normalized reproducing-kernel object. That could help you reframe some of the local algebra more canonically.

As for Connes specifically: I do not think Connes gives you an off-the-shelf fix for the current blockers. But he may give you a useful way to repackage the global side.

Connes’ program gives a spectral interpretation of the critical zeros as an absorption spectrum and interprets the explicit formula as a trace formula on the noncommutative space of adele classes. In his 1998 paper he explicitly presents the zeros this way, and in his recent survey he again describes the spectral side as zeros of LL-functions and the geometric side as contributions from places/primes.

Why that might matter for your architecture:

Your current machine is strongest on the local side: quotient, whitening, transverse scalar, calibration, odd-mode cancellation. Where it is weakest is the global bridge: universal finite-core exclusion, uniformity in scale, and the high-height closure. Connes is almost the mirror image: very global, very trace-formula/spectral, not a microscopic local rigidity tool. So the most plausible import is not “a theorem to paste in,” but a way to upgrade your endgame from an ad hoc discrete cancellation into something more canonical.

The most promising specific analogy is this:

Your NN-point odd-moment cancellation already looks like a finite-difference/test-function projector killing low odd modes. Connes’ framework is built around choosing test functions and reading off a spectral side versus a geometric side through a trace formula.
So one possible import is to reinterpret your coefficient package aj(N)aj(N)​ not merely as a combinatorial trick, but as the discrete shadow of a more canonical test-function construction. If that worked, it could help in three places:

    it might make the NN-point cancellation feel less bespoke and more invariant;

    it might give a cleaner route to the effective-threshold issue, because trace/test-function frameworks are naturally quantitative;

    it might help connect your local transverse anomaly to a genuinely global spectral obstruction, which is exactly where the current draft is weakest.

What I do not think Connes is likely to help with directly is the current hardest local problem: the 4-core finite geometry. His machinery is too global and too spectral for that. Your 3-point/4-point branch is about very local affine/circuit rigidity; Connes is about the global explicit-formula side on adele classes. Those are different scales of attack.

So my bottom line is:

    Yes, there are ingredients worth importing.

    The most concrete imports are from computational verification and derivative geometry.

    Connes is probably not a direct patch, but could be valuable as a global wrapper for your cancellation/calibration layer.

    The best-case role for Connes is to help turn your current discrete odd-mode projector into a more canonical spectral/test-function formalism.

    The worst-case risk is that trying to import Connes now just adds a second huge framework on top of the first one without solving the actual local core problem.

If I were choosing one import path to explore first, I would not start by trying to merge in all of Connes. I would start with a narrow question:

Can the NN-point cancellation operator be reinterpreted as a canonical test-function or projector, in a way that makes the endgame more global and effective?

That is the Connes-flavored import that seems most aligned with what your draft already does.

That is already very close in spirit to the Connes/Weil-test-function viewpoint, where one probes the zero set by feeding explicit test functions into a trace or explicit-formula functional. Connes’ 1998 framework explicitly interprets the zeros spectrally and the explicit formula geometrically as a trace formula on adele classes, while Connes–Consani’s later work studies test functions on R+×R+×​ and uses an operator QQ that multiplies the Fourier/Mellin side by z2+14z2+41​ precisely to impose vanishing at the boundary points ±i/2±i/2. Conrey’s RH survey presents the same general philosophy through Weil’s explicit formula and positivity criterion for compactly supported test functions.

That is the best mathematical version of the analogy I can see right now.

What this could buy you is not a miracle theorem, but a cleaner global wrapper.

First, it makes the coefficients aj(N)aj(N)​ look canonical. They are no longer “the weights that happened to work,” but the discrete test distribution supported at the microscopic multiplicative nodes ej/Q2ej/Q2 whose Mellin transform preserves the constant mode and annihilates low odd jets.

Second, it suggests a path from your discrete cancellation to an actual explicit-formula object. Instead of the raw distribution μN,QμN,Q​, define a smoothed compactly supported multiplicative test function

That is the step that makes the analogy potentially usable rather than merely poetic. This matches the general test-function setup in Weil/Connes style approaches, where the Mellin-side shape of the test function is the real object being designed.

Third, it may help with your effective-threshold problem. Right now the NN-point cancellation is proved by a direct Taylor-tail estimate. In a true test-function formalism, one would hope to encode the same cancellation in the symbol f^N,Q,ηf

what does this actually get us?

Three things, one conceptual and two potentially useful.

The conceptual gain is the biggest immediate one: it shows your NN-point operator is not an ad hoc finite-difference trick. It is a genuine discrete projector with an explicit Mellin-side symbol
μ^N,Q(z),
μ
​N,Q​(z),

and that symbol has a built-in design:

    it preserves the constant mode:
    μ^N,Q(0)=1;
    μ
    ​N,Q​(0)=1;

    it kills the first N−1N−1 odd jets:
    ∂z2r+1μ^N,Q(0)=0(0≤r≤N−2);
    ∂z2r+1​μ
    ​N,Q​(0)=0(0≤r≤N−2);

    its odd part starts only at order 2N−12N−1.

So the NN-point layer becomes conceptually cleaner: you are applying a local Mellin-side odd-jet annihilator to the corrected scalar germ. That is a much nicer story than “here are some coefficients that happen to cancel moments.”

The first concrete payoff is that it can give you cleaner proofs and cleaner constants.

Right now the paper proves things by expanding
Hm(s)=∑cnsn
Hm​(s)=∑cn​sn

and then summing moments
μn(N)=∑aj(N)jn.
μn(N)​=∑aj(N)​jn.

With the symbol, you can instead think of
Ξζ(N)(m)=PN,QHm
Ξζ(N)​(m)=PN,Q​Hm​

as applying a single operator whose odd part is explicitly controlled by
μ^N,Q(z)−μ^N,Q(−z).
μ
​N,Q​(z)−μ
​N,Q​(−z).

That may let you reprove the cancellation layer in a more invariant way, and possibly extract sharper explicit constants for the effective-threshold problem.

The second concrete payoff is that it gives you a plausible bridge to a test-function / explicit-formula formalism.

The symbol says exactly what frequencies you are suppressing near z=0z=0. That means you can try to smooth the discrete measure and build an honest multiplicative test function whose Mellin transform has essentially the same low-odd-jet vanishing. If that works, your NN-point cancellation stops looking like a local combinatorial gadget and starts looking like a canonical test-function projector. That is the one place where a Connes/Weil-style comparison could become mathematically useful rather than decorative.

So the positive answer is:

    it canonically explains the NN-point operator;

    it may simplify and sharpen the NN-point estimates;

    it may open a route to a more global test-function packaging.

But the honest negative answer is just as important:

by itself, it does not solve any of the current major open bridges.

It does not solve:

    pair-like versus genuine finite-core,

    the 4-core geometry,

    the variable-xx calibration rewrite,

    or the high-height-only issue.

So this is not “we derived the closed form, therefore the proof is now close.” It is more modest than that.

My best honest summary is:

It upgrades the NN-point cancellation from a clever trick to a structured operator.
That matters, because structured operators can often be generalized, optimized, and embedded into bigger frameworks.
But it is still a refinement of the cancellation layer, not a solution to the universal endgame.

If you want the most practical next use, it is this: try to use the symbol to get a sharper, more canonical version of the NN-point zeta estimate, with explicit constants. That is the place where it is most likely to pay off soon.

I’m pushing on the NN-point layer itself now: whether the explicit symbol lets us rewrite the current Taylor-tail proof into something cleaner and genuinely sharper, rather than just prettier notation.

Yes. There is a genuinely cleaner version of the NN-point zeta estimate hiding here.

The key move is to stop bounding the odd tail coefficient-by-coefficient and instead package the whole odd tail into a single exact rational generating function.

Let
μk(N):=∑j=1Naj(N)jk,
μk(N)​:=j=1∑N​aj(N)​jk,

and define the ordinary odd-moment generating function
MN(u):=∑r≥0μ2r+1(N)u2r+1=∑j=1Naj(N) j u1−j2u2,∣u∣<1N.
MN​(u):=r≥0∑​μ2r+1(N)​u2r+1=j=1∑N​1−j2u2aj(N)​ju​,∣u∣<N1​.

Then the symbol work gives a very clean exact identity:
MN(u)=(−1)N+1(2N−1)N!(N−1)!u2N−1∏j=1N(1−j2u2).
MN​(u)=(−1)N+1(2N−1)N!(N−1)!∏j=1N​(1−j2u2)u2N−1​.​

This is the right canonical replacement for the crude bound
∣μ2r+1(N)∣≤(2N−1)N2r+1.
∣μ2r+1(N)​∣≤(2N−1)N2r+1.

Why it is true:

Set
DN(u):=∏j=1N(1−j2u2) MN(u).
DN​(u):=j=1∏N​(1−j2u2)MN​(u).

Because MNMN​ is a sum of simple fractions, DN(u)DN​(u) is an odd polynomial of degree at most 2N−12N−1. But from the moment cancellations
μ2r+1(N)=0(0≤r≤N−2),
μ2r+1(N)​=0(0≤r≤N−2),

we know
MN(u)=O(u2N−1)(u→0),
MN​(u)=O(u2N−1)(u→0),

hence
DN(u)=CNu2N−1
DN​(u)=CN​u2N−1

for some constant CNCN​.

To determine CNCN​, use the exponential symbol. From the closed-form odd part
μ^N,Q(v)−μ^N,Q(−v)2=(−1)N+1N 22N−1(2N−2N−1)sinh⁡2N−1 ⁣(v2)cosh⁡ ⁣(v2),
2μ
​N,Q​(v)−μ
​N,Q​(−v)​=(−1)N+1(N−12N−2​)N22N−1​sinh2N−1(2v​)cosh(2v​),

the coefficient of v2N−1v2N−1 is
μ2N−1(N)(2N−1)!=(−1)N+1N(2N−2N−1).
(2N−1)!μ2N−1(N)​​=(−1)N+1(N−12N−2​)N​.

Therefore

First, insert the following block immediately after the proof of Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-coefficients}.

\begin{lemma}[Exact odd-moment generating function]
\label{lem:n-point-odd-generating-function}
Define
\[
\mu_k^{(N)}:=\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}j^k,
\]
and, for \(|u|<1/N\),
\[
\mathcal M_N(u)
:=
\sum_{r\ge 0}\mu_{2r+1}^{(N)}u^{2r+1}
=
\sum_{j=1}^N \frac{a_j^{(N)}\,j\,u}{1-j^2u^2}.
\]
Then
\[
\mathcal M_N(u)
=
(-1)^{N+1}(2N-1)N!(N-1)!
\frac{u^{2N-1}}{\prod_{j=1}^N(1-j^2u^2)}.
\]
In particular, for every real \(x>N\),
\[
\sum_{r\ge N-1} |\mu_{2r+1}^{(N)}|\,x^{-(2r+1)}
=
(2N-1)N!(N-1)!\,x^2\,
\frac{\Gamma(x-N)}{\Gamma(x+N+1)}.
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
The identity
\[
\mathcal M_N(u)
=
\sum_{j=1}^N \frac{a_j^{(N)}\,j\,u}{1-j^2u^2}
\]
is immediate from summing the geometric series
\[
\frac{j\,u}{1-j^2u^2}
=
\sum_{r\ge 0} j^{2r+1}u^{2r+1}
\qquad (|u|<1/j).
\]

Set
\[
D_N(u):=
\prod_{j=1}^N(1-j^2u^2)\,\mathcal M_N(u).
\]
Since \(\mathcal M_N\) is a sum of simple fractions, \(D_N(u)\) is an odd
polynomial of degree at most \(2N-1\). By
Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-coefficients},
\[
\mu_{2r+1}^{(N)}=0
\qquad (0\le r\le N-2),
\]
so \(\mathcal M_N(u)=O(u^{2N-1})\) as \(u\to 0\). Hence
\[
D_N(u)=C_N\,u^{2N-1}
\]
for some constant \(C_N\).

To determine \(C_N\), compare residues at \(u=1/N\). The residue of
\(\mathcal M_N(u)\) there comes only from the \(j=N\) term and equals
\[
\operatorname*{Res}_{u=1/N}\mathcal M_N(u)
=
-\frac{a_N^{(N)}}{2N}.
\]
On the other hand,
\[
\operatorname*{Res}_{u=1/N}
\frac{C_Nu^{2N-1}}{\prod_{j=1}^N(1-j^2u^2)}
=
-\frac{C_N}{2N^2\prod_{k=1}^{N-1}(1-k^2/N^2)}.
\]
Equating these residues gives
\[
C_N
=
a_N^{(N)}\,N\prod_{k=1}^{N-1}(N^2-k^2).
\]
Now
\[
a_N^{(N)}
=
(-1)^{N+1}\frac{N}{\binom{2N-2}{N-1}},
\]
and
\[
\prod_{k=1}^{N-1}(N^2-k^2)
=
\prod_{k=1}^{N-1}(N-k)\prod_{k=1}^{N-1}(N+k)
=
(N-1)!\,\frac{(2N-1)!}{N!}
=
\frac{(2N-1)!}{N}.
\]
Therefore
\[
C_N
=
(-1)^{N+1}\frac{N(2N-1)!}{\binom{2N-2}{N-1}}
=
(-1)^{N+1}(2N-1)N!(N-1)!.
\]
This proves the first formula.

For the tail identity, note that
\[
(-1)^{N+1}\mathcal M_N(u)
=
(2N-1)N!(N-1)!
\frac{u^{2N-1}}{\prod_{j=1}^N(1-j^2u^2)}
\]
has nonnegative Taylor coefficients for \(0<u<1/N\). Hence
\[
(-1)^{N+1}\mu_{2r+1}^{(N)}\ge 0
\qquad (r\ge N-1),
\]
and therefore, for \(x>N\),
\[
\sum_{r\ge N-1} |\mu_{2r+1}^{(N)}|\,x^{-(2r+1)}
=
\left|\mathcal M_N\!\left(\frac1x\right)\right|
=
(2N-1)N!(N-1)!\,
\frac{x^2}{\prod_{j=1}^N(x^2-j^2)}.
\]
Finally,
\[
\prod_{j=1}^N(x^2-j^2)
=
\prod_{j=1}^N(x-j)\prod_{j=1}^N(x+j)
=
\frac{\Gamma(x)}{\Gamma(x-N)}\cdot
\frac{\Gamma(x+N+1)}{\Gamma(x+1)}
=
\frac{1}{x}\frac{\Gamma(x+N+1)}{\Gamma(x-N)}.
\]
Substituting this identity yields the claimed formula.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Corrected $N$-point transverse estimate for the full corrected scalar]
\label{prop:corrected-n-point}
...
\end{proof}

with this:

\begin{proposition}[Kernel form and explicit corrected $N$-point transverse estimate]
\label{prop:corrected-n-point}
Let
\[
H_m(s):=\Phi_K\!\bigl(\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m)\bigr)
\]
be the full corrected transverse scalar.
For \(N=\lfloor \alpha Q\rfloor\) with \(0<\alpha<\rho_0\), define
\[
s_j=\frac{j}{Q^2},
\qquad
\Xi_\zeta^{(N)}(m)
:=
\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}\,H_m(s_j).
\]
Then
\[
\Xi_\zeta^{(N)}(m)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\oint_{|\xi|=\rho_0/Q}
H_m(\xi)\,K_{N,Q}(\xi)\,d\xi,
\]
where
\[
K_{N,Q}(\xi)
:=
2\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}\frac{s_j}{\xi^2-s_j^2}
=
2(-1)^{N+1}(2N-1)N!(N-1)! \, Q^4\xi\,
\frac{\Gamma(Q^2\xi-N)}{\Gamma(Q^2\xi+N+1)}.
\]
Moreover, writing \(x:=\rho_0Q\), one has the explicit bound
\[
|\Xi_\zeta^{(N)}(m)|
\ll_{\rho_0}
\rho_0^2\,
\frac{(2N-1)N!(N-1)!}{Q}\,
\frac{\Gamma(x-N)}{\Gamma(x+N+1)}\,
L(m)S(m)^2.
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
By Proposition~\ref{prop:corrected-whitening}, the full corrected scalar
\[
H_m(s)=\Phi_K\!\bigl(\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m)\bigr)
\]
is holomorphic on \(|s|<\rho_0/Q\). By
Proposition~\ref{prop:cutoff-compatibility}, all cutoff-dependent tail,
jet-correction, and estimation contributions are already internal to the full
corrected scalar \(H_m(s)\); hence no external remainder is carried into
\(\Xi_\zeta^{(N)}(m)\).

Since \(H_m\) is odd, the odd Cauchy formula gives
\[
H_m(s)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}\oint_{|\xi|=\rho_0/Q}
H_m(\xi)\,\frac{2s}{\xi^2-s^2}\,d\xi.
\]
Summing this identity against the coefficients \(a_j^{(N)}\) at
\(s=s_j=j/Q^2\) yields
\[
\Xi_\zeta^{(N)}(m)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}\oint_{|\xi|=\rho_0/Q}
H_m(\xi)\,K_{N,Q}(\xi)\,d\xi
\]
with
\[
K_{N,Q}(\xi)
=
2\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}\frac{s_j}{\xi^2-s_j^2}.
\]

Now
\[
\frac{s_j}{\xi^2-s_j^2}
=
\frac{1}{\xi}\,
\frac{j/(Q^2\xi)}{1-j^2/(Q^4\xi^2)},
\]
so, using Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-odd-generating-function},
\[
K_{N,Q}(\xi)
=
\frac{2}{\xi}\,
\mathcal M_N\!\left(\frac{1}{Q^2\xi}\right)
=
2(-1)^{N+1}(2N-1)N!(N-1)!\,
\frac{Q^2}{\prod_{j=1}^N(Q^4\xi^2-j^2)}.
\]
Finally,
\[
\prod_{j=1}^N(Q^4\xi^2-j^2)
=
\prod_{j=1}^N(Q^2\xi-j)\prod_{j=1}^N(Q^2\xi+j)
=
\frac{1}{Q^2\xi}\,
\frac{\Gamma(Q^2\xi+N+1)}{\Gamma(Q^2\xi-N)},
\]
which gives
\[
K_{N,Q}(\xi)
=
2(-1)^{N+1}(2N-1)N!(N-1)! \, Q^4\xi\,
\frac{\Gamma(Q^2\xi-N)}{\Gamma(Q^2\xi+N+1)}.
\]

For the size bound, Proposition~\ref{prop:boundary-estimate} gives
\[
|H_m(s)|\ll_{\rho_0}\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q^3}
\qquad (|s|=\rho_0/Q).
\]
By Proposition~\ref{prop:odd-expansion}, one may write
\[
H_m(z/Q^2)
=
\sum_{r\ge 0} c_{2r+1}(m)\frac{z^{2r+1}}{Q^{2r+4}},
\qquad
|c_{2r+1}(m)|\ll L(m)S(m)^2\,\rho_0^{-(2r+1)}.
\]
Therefore
\[
\Xi_\zeta^{(N)}(m)
=
\sum_{r\ge 0} c_{2r+1}(m)\frac{\mu_{2r+1}^{(N)}}{Q^{2r+4}}.
\]
By Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-coefficients},
\[
\mu_{2r+1}^{(N)}=0
\qquad (0\le r\le N-2),
\]
hence
\[
|\Xi_\zeta^{(N)}(m)|
\ll_{\rho_0}
\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q^3}
\sum_{r\ge N-1}
|\mu_{2r+1}^{(N)}|\,(\rho_0Q)^{-(2r+1)}.
\]
Applying Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-odd-generating-function} with \(x=\rho_0Q\)
gives
\[
\sum_{r\ge N-1}
|\mu_{2r+1}^{(N)}|\,(\rho_0Q)^{-(2r+1)}
=
(2N-1)N!(N-1)!(\rho_0Q)^2
\frac{\Gamma(\rho_0Q-N)}{\Gamma(\rho_0Q+N+1)}.
\]
Substituting this proves the stated bound.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Optimized zeta-side cancellation depth]
\label{cor:optimized-zeta-cancellation-depth}
Let
\[
N=\lfloor \alpha Q\rfloor,
\qquad
0<\alpha<\rho_0,
\qquad
\beta:=\frac{\alpha}{\rho_0}\in(0,1).
\]
Then
\[
|\Xi_\zeta^{(N)}(m)|
\ll_{\rho_0,\alpha}
\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q}\,
e^{-\delta_*(\alpha,\rho_0)Q},
\]
where
\[
\delta_*(\alpha,\rho_0)
=
\rho_0\Bigl(
(1+\beta)\log(1+\beta)
-2\beta\log\beta
-(1-\beta)\log(1-\beta)
\Bigr).
\]
Moreover, \(\delta_*(\alpha,\rho_0)\) is maximized uniquely at
\[
\beta=\frac{1}{\sqrt2},
\qquad\text{i.e.}\qquad
\alpha_{\mathrm{opt}}=\frac{\rho_0}{\sqrt2},
\]
so the optimal zeta-side cancellation depth is
\[
N_{\mathrm{opt}}
=
\left\lfloor \frac{\rho_0}{\sqrt2}\,Q\right\rfloor,
\]
and the corresponding exponential rate is
\[
\delta_{\mathrm{opt}}
=
2\rho_0\log(1+\sqrt2).
\]
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
Set \(x:=\rho_0Q\), so that \(N=\beta x+O(1)\) with \(\beta\in(0,1)\) fixed.
By Proposition~\ref{prop:corrected-n-point}, it suffices to estimate
\[
(2N-1)N!(N-1)!\,\frac{\Gamma(x-N)}{\Gamma(x+N+1)}.
\]
A Stirling expansion gives
\[
\log\!\left(
(2N-1)N!(N-1)!\,\frac{\Gamma(x-N)}{\Gamma(x+N+1)}
\right)
=
-x\,f(\beta)+O(\log x),
\]
where
\[
f(\beta)
=
(1+\beta)\log(1+\beta)
-2\beta\log\beta
-(1-\beta)\log(1-\beta).
\]
Hence
\[
(2N-1)N!(N-1)!\,\frac{\Gamma(x-N)}{\Gamma(x+N+1)}
\ll_{\rho_0,\alpha}
e^{-x f(\beta)},
\]
and therefore
\[
|\Xi_\zeta^{(N)}(m)|
\ll_{\rho_0,\alpha}
\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q}\,
e^{-\rho_0 f(\beta)Q}.
\]
This proves the first claim.

For the optimization, differentiate:
\[
f'(\beta)
=
\log\!\left(\frac{1-\beta^2}{\beta^2}\right).
\]
Thus \(f'(\beta)=0\) exactly when \(\beta=1/\sqrt2\). Since
\[
f''(\beta)
=
-\frac{2\beta}{1-\beta^2}-\frac{2}{\beta}<0
\qquad (0<\beta<1),
\]
this critical point is the unique maximizer. Finally,
\[
f\!\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\right)
=
2\log(1+\sqrt2),
\]
which gives the stated value of \(\delta_{\mathrm{opt}}\).
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}
Corollary~\ref{cor:optimized-zeta-cancellation-depth} optimizes the
\emph{zeta-side} cancellation depth. In the full zeta--toy comparison one must
also satisfy the toy-side restriction \(0<\alpha<\rho_{\toy}\) from
Proposition~\ref{prop:toy-n-point-direct}. Accordingly, the common admissible
choice is any
\[
\alpha<\min\!\left\{\rho_{\toy},\frac{\rho_0}{\sqrt2}\right\},
\]
and if \(\rho_{\toy}>\rho_0/\sqrt2\), one may take the exact zeta-side optimum
\(\alpha=\rho_0/\sqrt2\).
\end{remark}

One tiny cleanup after this replacement: if you want the introduction and later summaries to reflect the stronger result, phrases like “for some δ>0δ>0” can now be sharpened to point to Corollary~\ref{cor:optimized-zeta-cancellation-depth} instead.

That captures the genuine mathematical gains without making the paper sound like it is importing a second huge framework. Connes’s trace-formula program really does give a spectral interpretation of zeros and a geometric interpretation of the explicit formula, while Conrey’s survey is a good standard reference for the explicit-formula / test-function viewpoint; the Connes–Consani archimedean paper is a reasonable citation for the test-function/positivity side.

Here are the insertions.

First, in the Introduction, insert this immediately after the existing paragraph that begins For orientation, it may be helpful to situate the present framework... and ends with ...subsumes those earlier viewpoints.

\paragraph{A Mellin-side interpretation of the $N$-point layer.}
A second point of orientation concerns the $N$-point cancellation operator
introduced later in the proof.  The cancellation coefficients may be viewed as
defining a discrete multiplicative distribution whose Mellin transform preserves
the constant mode while annihilating the first \(N-1\) odd jets.  In this
sense, the $N$-point step is not merely a finite-difference device: it may be
read as a local Mellin-side projector acting on the corrected transverse germ.
This is formally reminiscent of the Weil/Connes test-function viewpoint, in
which one probes the zero set through Mellin/Fourier transforms of
multiplicative test functions and explicit- or trace-formula pairings
\cite{Conrey2003,Connes1997,ConnesConsani2021}.  The present argument does
\emph{not} invoke any global trace formula, positivity principle, or spectral
realization theorem from that literature; the comparison is included only as
heuristic orientation.  What is used here is a local, explicit, finite-support
projector whose Mellin symbol can be written in closed form and whose odd part
starts only at order \(2N-1\).

Second, in the section \section{\texorpdfstring{$N$}{N}-point odd-moment cancellation}, insert the following immediately after the proof of Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-coefficients} and before Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-odd-generating-function}.

\begin{lemma}[Closed-form Mellin symbol of the discrete $N$-point projector]
\label{lem:n-point-mellin-symbol}
Define
\[
A_N(t):=\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}\,t^j,
\qquad
\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(z):=A_N(e^{z/Q^2}).
\]
Then
\[
A_N(t)
=
\frac{2(2N-1)}{N+1}\,
t\,{}_2F_1(1-N,\,2;\,N+2;\,t),
\]
so in particular
\[
\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(z)
=
\frac{2(2N-1)}{N+1}\,
e^{z/Q^2}\,
{}_2F_1(1-N,\,2;\,N+2;\,e^{z/Q^2}).
\]
Moreover,
\[
A_N(t)-A_N(t^{-1})
=
\frac{N(-1)^N}{\binom{2N-2}{N-1}}\,
t^{-N}(1-t)^{2N-1}(1+t),
\]
and hence
\[
\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(z)-\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(-z)
=
\frac{N(-1)^N}{\binom{2N-2}{N-1}}\,
e^{-Nz/Q^2}\,
\bigl(1-e^{z/Q^2}\bigr)^{2N-1}
\bigl(1+e^{z/Q^2}\bigr).
\]
In particular,
\[
\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(0)=1,
\qquad
\partial_z^{\,2r+1}\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(0)=0
\quad (0\le r\le N-2),
\]
and the odd part of \(\widehat\mu_{N,Q}\) starts only at order \(2N-1\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Let
\[
B_N(t):=
\frac{2(2N-1)}{N+1}\,
t\,{}_2F_1(1-N,\,2;\,N+2;\,t)
=
\sum_{j=1}^N b_j^{(N)}\,t^j.
\]
Its coefficients satisfy
\[
\frac{b_{j+1}^{(N)}}{b_j^{(N)}}
=
\frac{(1-N+j-1)(2+j-1)}{(N+2+j-1)j}
=
\frac{(j-N)(j+1)}{j(N+j+1)}.
\]
On the other hand, the coefficients \(a_j^{(N)}\) satisfy
\[
\frac{a_{j+1}^{(N)}}{a_j^{(N)}}
=
\frac{(-1)^{j+2}(j+1)\binom{2N}{N+j+1}}
     {(-1)^{j+1}j\binom{2N}{N+j}}
=
\frac{(j-N)(j+1)}{j(N+j+1)}.
\]
Thus \(a_j^{(N)}\) and \(b_j^{(N)}\) agree up to an overall constant.  Matching
the \(t^1\)-coefficient gives
\[
b_1^{(N)}
=
\frac{2(2N-1)}{N+1}
=
\frac{\binom{2N}{N+1}}{\binom{2N-2}{N-1}}
=
a_1^{(N)},
\]
hence \(A_N(t)=B_N(t)\).

For the second identity, define
\[
F_N(t):=\sum_{j=-N}^{N}(-1)^j\binom{2N}{N+j}t^j.
\]
By the binomial theorem,
\[
F_N(t)=(-1)^N t^{-N}(1-t)^{2N}.
\]
Differentiating gives
\[
tF_N'(t)
=
\sum_{j=-N}^{N}j(-1)^j\binom{2N}{N+j}t^j
=
-N(-1)^N t^{-N}(1-t)^{2N-1}(1+t).
\]
Now
\[
A_N(t)-A_N(t^{-1})
=
-\frac{1}{\binom{2N-2}{N-1}}
\sum_{j=1}^N j(-1)^j\binom{2N}{N+j}(t^j-t^{-j}),
\]
while the symmetry \(j\mapsto -j\) in the preceding display shows
\[
tF_N'(t)
=
\sum_{j=1}^N j(-1)^j\binom{2N}{N+j}(t^j-t^{-j}).
\]
Substituting the closed form for \(tF_N'(t)\) yields
\[
A_N(t)-A_N(t^{-1})
=
\frac{N(-1)^N}{\binom{2N-2}{N-1}}\,
t^{-N}(1-t)^{2N-1}(1+t).
\]
Replacing \(t\) by \(e^{z/Q^2}\) gives the displayed formula for
\(\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(z)-\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(-z)\).  The normalization
\(\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(0)=1\) and the low odd-jet vanishing are equivalent to the
moment identities in Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-coefficients}.
\end{proof}

Third, insert this immediately after Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-odd-generating-function} and its proof.

\begin{remark}[Structure of the surviving odd tail]
\label{rem:surviving-odd-tail-structure}
Expanding
\[
\frac{1}{\prod_{j=1}^N(1-j^2u^2)}
=
\sum_{k\ge 0} h_k(1^2,\dots,N^2)\,u^{2k},
\]
where \(h_k\) denotes the complete homogeneous symmetric polynomial, the exact
formula of Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-odd-generating-function} yields
\[
\mu_{2N-1+2k}^{(N)}
=
(-1)^{N+1}(2N-1)N!(N-1)!\,
h_k(1^2,\dots,N^2),
\qquad k\ge 0.
\]
Thus the surviving odd moments are not merely bounded: they are explicit and
have a fixed sign,
\[
(-1)^{N+1}\mu_{2r+1}^{(N)}\ge 0
\qquad (r\ge N-1).
\]
This makes the post-cancellation odd tail completely structured rather than
mysterious.
\end{remark}

Fourth, insert this immediately after the current remark following Corollary~\ref{cor:optimized-zeta-cancellation-depth} — the one that ends with $\alpha=\rho_0/\sqrt2$.

\begin{remark}[Discrete Mellin projector and test-function viewpoint]
\label{rem:discrete-mellin-projector}
Define the additive operator
\[
(\mathcal P_{N,Q}F):=\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}\,F\!\left(\frac{j}{Q^2}\right),
\]
and the associated multiplicative distribution on \(\mathbb R_+^\times\),
\[
\mu_{N,Q}:=\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}\,\delta_{e^{j/Q^2}}.
\]
Its Mellin symbol is precisely
\[
\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(z)=\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}e^{zj/Q^2}.
\]
Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-mellin-symbol} shows that this symbol preserves the
constant mode and kills the first \(N-1\) odd jets at \(z=0\).  Thus
\(\mathcal P_{N,Q}\) acts on a general analytic germ as evaluation at \(0\)
plus even leakage and a high odd tail, while on an odd germ it is a
high-order odd-jet annihilator.

In this sense, the $N$-point operator may be viewed as a local discrete
Mellin-side projector.  This is formally reminiscent of the Weil/Connes
test-function viewpoint, where one probes the zero set through Mellin/Fourier
transforms of multiplicative test functions and explicit- or trace-formula
pairings \cite{Conrey2003,Connes1997,ConnesConsani2021}.  The present paper
does not use any global trace formula; the comparison is only structural.

A natural smoothed version is obtained by fixing an even
\(\phi\in C_c^\infty(\mathbb R)\) with \(\widehat\phi(0)=1\) and setting
\[
f_{N,Q,\eta}(x)
:=
\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}
\,\eta^{-1}\phi\!\left(\frac{\log x-j/Q^2}{\eta}\right).
\]
Its Mellin transform is
\[
\widehat f_{N,Q,\eta}(z)
=
\widehat\phi(\eta z)\,\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(z).
\]
Hence the same low odd-jet annihilation persists after smoothing, while the
finite-support projector is replaced by an honest smooth multiplicative test
function.  This observation is not used elsewhere in the proof, but it places
the $N$-point cancellation layer in a more canonical operator-theoretic
setting.
\end{remark}

Finally, add these bibliography items before \end{thebibliography}.

\bibitem{Connes1997}
A.~Connes,
\newblock Trace formula in noncommutative geometry and the zeros of the Riemann zeta function,
\newblock \emph{Journ\'ees \'Equations aux d\'eriv\'ees partielles} (1997), Art.~No.~4, 1--28.

\bibitem{ConnesConsani2021}
A.~Connes and C.~Consani,
\newblock Weil positivity and trace formula: the archimedean place,
\newblock arXiv:2006.13771, 2021.

Two small recommendations.

The first is to keep all of this as remarks/orientation except for the closed-form symbol lemma. That lemma is a real structural theorem. The rest is useful framing, but it should stay explicitly nonessential.

The second is to avoid saying Connes “solves” or “explains” your endgame. The honest value here is that your NN-point coefficients now look like a canonical Mellin-side projector with an explicit symbol, and that makes the operator feel far less ad hoc. That is the right gain to claim.

\begin{remark}[Discrete Mellin projector and test-function viewpoint]

insert the following immediately after

\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(z)=\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}e^{zj/Q^2}.

Equivalently, if \(D:=\frac{d}{ds}\), then
\[
\mathcal P_{N,Q}
=
P_{N,Q}(D)\big|_{s=0},
\qquad
P_{N,Q}(D):=\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}e^{(j/Q^2)D}.
\]
Thus \(\mathcal P_{N,Q}\) is a polynomial in the translation semigroup,

\[

\]
then
\[

=

\mu_n^{(N)}:=\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}j^n.
\]
Accordingly, on a general analytic germ \(\mathcal P_{N,Q}\) acts as

\begin{remark}[Sharper prefactor in the optimized zeta-side bound]
\label{rem:optimized-zeta-prefactor}
With the notation of Corollary~\ref{cor:optimized-zeta-cancellation-depth},
set
\[
x:=\rho_0Q,
\qquad
N=\beta x+O(1),
\qquad
\beta=\frac{\alpha}{\rho_0}\in(0,1).
\]
A more precise Stirling expansion of the gamma-ratio factor in
Proposition~\ref{prop:corrected-n-point} gives
\[
(2N-1)N!(N-1)!\,\frac{\Gamma(x-N)}{\Gamma(x+N+1)}
=
\frac{4\pi\beta}{\sqrt{1-\beta^2}}\,
e^{-x f(\beta)}\,
(1+o(1)),
\]
where
\[
f(\beta)
=
(1+\beta)\log(1+\beta)
-2\beta\log\beta
-(1-\beta)\log(1-\beta).
\]
Consequently,
\[
|\Xi_\zeta^{(N)}(m)|
\ll
2C_{\partial}(\rho_0)\rho_0\,
\frac{4\pi\beta}{\sqrt{1-\beta^2}}\,
\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q^2}\,
e^{-\delta_*(\alpha,\rho_0)Q}\,
(1+o(1)).
\]
At the zeta-side optimum \(\beta=1/\sqrt2\), the prefactor simplifies to
\(4\pi\), so
\[
|\Xi_\zeta^{(N_{\mathrm{opt}})}(m)|
\ll
8\pi\,C_{\partial}(\rho_0)\rho_0\,
\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q^2}\,
e^{-2\rho_0\log(1+\sqrt2)\,Q}\,
(1+o(1)).
\]
This sharper prefactor is not needed for the qualitative contradiction, but it
records that, once the boundary constant \(C_{\partial}(\rho_0)\) is made
explicit, the zeta-side \(N\)-point estimate becomes effective with an
explicit leading constant.
\end{remark}

here's the updated draft. now, let's probe what we can further gain from both the derivative-geometry import speculation and stronger Connes discussion

I’m looking at the updated draft’s Connes/Mellin and finite-core sections first, so I can say what further gains are realistic rather than just tossing in new speculative material.

Yes. The speculative directions in your note are sensible, and they split pretty cleanly into two different kinds of possible gain.

The important distinction is this: derivative geometry is the only one that plausibly attacks the remaining finite-core bottleneck, while stronger Connes discussion mostly helps canonize the NN-point layer, globalize the language, and maybe improve effectivity. Connes’s 1998 program interprets the critical zeros spectrally and the explicit formula geometrically as a trace formula on adele classes, while Connes–Consani’s later work ties Weil positivity to a compressed scaling action and uses Hermitian Toeplitz matrices to control the difference term. Separately, the derivative-geometry line really does encode zeta-zero information through zeros of derivatives; that is exactly the kind of bridge your current finite-core branch would need if it is to connect local lifted-circuit geometry to a more classical obstruction.

What you can further gain from derivative-geometry import is not a new intro paragraph so much as a new target theorem. The most promising version would be: a shrinking exact three- or four-point lifted circuit forces vanishing of a derivative-based local invariant of the lifted curve, and that vanishing in turn forces a nearby forbidden packet of derivative zeros. In your current language, the three-point side already looks like tangent-line contact, and the four-point side looks like higher contact / affine-circuit contact; that strongly suggests trying to attach a projective-curvature, pre-Schwarzian, or slope/curvature-type invariant to the lifted curve and show that exact circuit sheets can only persist when that invariant vanishes in a way that is visible to derivative geometry. That would be valuable because it would externalize the finite-core obstruction: instead of fighting circuits only inside your bespoke lifted coordinates, you would force them to manifest as a classical critical-point phenomenon. The likely payoff is highest on the mixed/nonmixed four-point side, where the present local algebra is richest but least closed.

There is a second, slightly weaker gain from derivative geometry: it could give you a source-side no-return criterion. If a bad lifted branch necessarily drags along a nearby ζ′ζ′- or ξ′ξ′-critical configuration, then one might try to rule out repeated return or recurrence of that branch by a classical derivative-zero geometry argument instead of by a bespoke lifted argument. That feels more realistic than hoping derivative geometry will directly prove the four-point exclusion by itself. In other words: derivative geometry is most promising as a bridge out of your local finite-core picture, not as a replacement for it.

What you can further gain from a stronger Connes discussion is different. Here the most meaningful step is to upgrade the current Mellin-side remarks from “nice analogy” to a more systematic test-function formalism. You already have the raw ingredients: a finite-support multiplicative distribution μN,QμN,Q​, its closed Mellin symbol, a smoothed test function fN,Q,ηfN,Q,η​, and the gamma-ratio kernel theorem. That is enough to say something sharper than the draft currently says: namely, that your NN-point operator is not only a discrete Mellin projector, but a microlocal test-function packet whose symbol is designed to preserve the constant mode and annihilate low odd jets. Connes’s framework is explicitly about choosing multiplicative test functions and reading spectral-versus-geometric information through a trace formula, and Connes–Consani’s later positivity analysis is explicitly organized around compressed scaling action and Toeplitz control. That makes your current NN-point layer feel much less ad hoc than a plain moment-cancellation trick.

The strongest concrete gain available there is probably effectivization, not closure of the local geometry. The current draft already has the kernel theorem and optimized depth. A stronger Connes-flavored discussion could motivate two next technical steps: first, prove a clean smoothing lemma for fN,Q,ηfN,Q,η​ that preserves the low odd-jet annihilation with explicit constants; second, study the associated Toeplitz/Hankel moment matrices of the smoothed symbol as a quantitative diagnostic. That would not solve the four-point branch, but it could make the NN-point layer feel canonical enough to support an explicit-threshold theorem later. The Connes–Consani paper is particularly suggestive here because its archimedean positivity discussion really does run through compressed scaling and Hermitian Toeplitz matrices.

What I do not think you gain from stronger Connes discussion is a direct fix for the current bottleneck. Your unresolved obstruction is still local finite-core geometry, especially the four-point side. Connes is global, spectral, and trace-formula-driven. So the honest best-case division of labor is: derivative geometry for the finite-core bridge; Connes/Mellin for the global wrapper and effectivity.

My recommendation for the paper itself is conservative. I would not push either direction much harder in the introduction. The draft already has about the right amount of orientation there. The right place for any further addition is a short end-of-paper outlook subsection with two paragraphs: one saying that a plausible next bridge is to convert shrinking lifted circuits into forbidden derivative-zero packets; the other saying that the Mellin projector suggests a future test-function/trace-formula packaging and possibly an effective threshold. That adds value without making the theorem spine look more ambitious than the mathematics beneath it.

My actual research recommendation is sharper: if you want one import direction to push mathematically rather than rhetorically, I would push derivative geometry first. Connes can make the NN-point layer prettier and perhaps more effective. Derivative geometry is the one that might actually bite into the remaining universal gap.

Here is the clean general statement.

1. Osculating-determinant bridge

shrinking exact nonmixed lifted 4-point branch ⟹Tv(h0)=0,
shrinking exact nonmixed lifted 4-point branch ⟹Tv​(h0​)=0,

and hence on Kv≠0Kv​=0,
R(h0)=0.
R(h0​)=0.

Then one more proposition:
Tv≡0 on a nonmixed interval ⟹Y=γS+ax+b.
Tv​≡0 on a nonmixed interval ⟹Y=γS+ax+b.

That is the cleanest derivative-geometry gain available from where the draft stands.

This matters because it converts the finite-core 44-point geometry into a problem about zeros and persistence of a single scalar derivative invariant RR, which is much closer in spirit to the classical derivative-geometry world than raw coplanarity sheets.

1) Insert this immediately before

\begin{proposition}[Shrinking exact lifted $3$-point circuits concentrate into $K_v=0$]

\begin{lemma}[Osculating determinant expansion for shrinking affine circuits]
\label{lem:osculating-determinant-expansion}
Let \(Q:I\to \mathbf C^d\) be real-analytic on an interval \(I\), and define
\[
\Delta_{d+1}(t_0,\dots,t_d)
:=
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1 & Q(t_0)\\
\vdots & \vdots\\
1 & Q(t_d)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Fix \(h_*\in I\), and let
\[
t_j=h_*+\varepsilon \lambda_j
\qquad (j=0,\dots,d),
\]
where the \(\lambda_j\) are pairwise distinct and \(\varepsilon\to 0\). Then
\[
\Delta_{d+1}(t_0,\dots,t_d)
=
\frac{\varepsilon^{d(d+1)/2}}{\prod_{k=1}^d k!}\,
V(\lambda_0,\dots,\lambda_d)\,
\det\!\bigl(Q'(h_*),Q''(h_*),\dots,Q^{(d)}(h_*)\bigr)
+
O\!\left(\varepsilon^{d(d+1)/2+1}\right),
\]
where
\[
V(\lambda_0,\dots,\lambda_d):=\prod_{0\le i<j\le d}(\lambda_j-\lambda_i)
\]
is the Vandermonde determinant.

In particular, if there is a sequence of exact affine \((d+1)\)-circuits
\[
\Delta_{d+1}(t_{0,n},\dots,t_{d,n})=0
\]
with pairwise distinct nodes \(t_{j,n}\to h_*\), then
\[
\det\!\bigl(Q'(h_*),Q''(h_*),\dots,Q^{(d)}(h_*)\bigr)=0.
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Write the Taylor expansion
\[
Q(h_*+\varepsilon\lambda)
=
Q(h_*)+\sum_{m=1}^d \frac{\varepsilon^m\lambda^m}{m!}\,Q^{(m)}(h_*)
+O(\varepsilon^{d+1}),
\]
uniformly for \(\lambda\) in bounded sets. Subtract the first row
\((1,Q(t_0))\) from each of the remaining rows. The first column then becomes
\((1,0,\dots,0)^{\top}\), so \(\Delta_{d+1}\) reduces to the determinant of the
\(d\times d\) matrix whose \(j\)-th row (\(j=1,\dots,d\)) is
\[
Q(t_j)-Q(t_0)
=
\sum_{m=1}^d \frac{\varepsilon^m(\lambda_j^m-\lambda_0^m)}{m!}\,Q^{(m)}(h_*)
+O(\varepsilon^{d+1}).
\]
Factor the column vectors \(Q'(h_*),\dots,Q^{(d)}(h_*)\). This gives
\[
\Delta_{d+1}(t_0,\dots,t_d)
=
\det\!\bigl(Q'(h_*),\dots,Q^{(d)}(h_*)\bigr)\,
\det\!\Bigl(\frac{\varepsilon^m(\lambda_j^m-\lambda_0^m)}{m!}\Bigr)_{1\le j,m\le d}
+O\!\left(\varepsilon^{d(d+1)/2+1}\right).
\]
Pulling \(\varepsilon^m/m!\) from the \(m\)-th column yields the factor
\[
\frac{\varepsilon^{1+\cdots+d}}{\prod_{k=1}^d k!}
=
\frac{\varepsilon^{d(d+1)/2}}{\prod_{k=1}^d k!}.
\]
The remaining determinant is the standard Vandermonde determinant
\(V(\lambda_0,\dots,\lambda_d)\). This proves the expansion.

If \(\Delta_{d+1}(t_{0,n},\dots,t_{d,n})=0\) for a sequence of shrinking
distinct-node circuits, divide by the nonzero Vandermonde factor and let
\(\varepsilon\to 0\). The leading term must vanish, giving
\[
\det\!\bigl(Q'(h_*),Q''(h_*),\dots,Q^{(d)}(h_*)\bigr)=0.
\qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Planar curvature and lifted torsion as the first derivative obstructions]
\label{rem:derivative-geometry-curvature-torsion-bridge}
Lemma~\ref{lem:osculating-determinant-expansion} shows that shrinking exact
affine circuits are controlled, at first nontrivial order, by derivative-side
osculating determinants. For a planar curve \(q\), shrinking exact collinear
triples force
\[
\det(q',q'')=0,
\]
and for a space curve \(Q\), shrinking exact coplanar quadruples force
\[
\det(Q',Q'',Q''')=0.
\]
Thus the defect-free lifted \(3\)-point and \(4\)-point branches are not merely
incidence problems: they are rooted at curvature- and torsion-type derivative
invariants of the corresponding lifted curves.
\end{remark}

2) Replace the proof of Proposition

\begin{proposition}[Shrinking exact lifted $3$-point circuits concentrate into $K_v=0$]

with this shorter proof:

\begin{proof}
Apply Lemma~\ref{lem:osculating-determinant-expansion} with \(d=2\) to the
planar lifted curve
\[
q(x):=(x,S(x)).
\]
The exact collinearity condition in the statement is precisely the vanishing of
\[
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1&x_1&S(x_1)\\
1&x_2&S(x_2)\\
1&x_3&S(x_3)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Hence shrinking exact lifted \(3\)-point circuits force
\[
\det(q'(x_*),q''(x_*))=0.
\]
Since
\[
q'(x)=(1,S'(x)),
\qquad
q''(x)=(0,S''(x)),
\]
one gets
\[
\det(q'(x_*),q''(x_*))=S''(x_*).
\]
On the \(x\)-patch, Proposition~\ref{prop:exact-x-coordinate-identities-lifted-four-point}
gives
\[
K_v=(x')^3S'',
\]
and \(x'\neq 0\) on a good \(v_5\)-patch. Therefore \(S''(x_*)=0\) implies
\[
K_v(x_*)=0.
\qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

3) Replace the proof of Proposition

\begin{proposition}[Shrinking exact lifted $4$-point circuits concentrate into $T_v=0$]

with this:

\begin{proof}
Apply Lemma~\ref{lem:osculating-determinant-expansion} with \(d=3\) to the
lifted space curve
\[
Q(x):=(Y(x),x,S(x)).
\]
The exact coplanarity condition in the statement is precisely the vanishing of
\[
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
1&Y(x_1)&x_1&S(x_1)\\
1&Y(x_2)&x_2&S(x_2)\\
1&Y(x_3)&x_3&S(x_3)\\
1&Y(x_4)&x_4&S(x_4)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Hence shrinking exact lifted \(4\)-point circuits force
\[
\det(Q'(x_*),Q''(x_*),Q'''(x_*))=0.
\]
But
\[
\det(Q'(x),Q''(x),Q'''(x))
=
W_x(1,Y,x,S)(x),
\]
and Proposition~\ref{prop:exact-x-coordinate-identities-lifted-four-point}
gives
\[
T_v=(x')^6\,W_x(1,Y,x,S).
\]
Since \(x'\neq 0\) on a good \(v_5\)-patch, it follows that
\[
T_v(x_*)=0.
\qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

and before the remark

\begin{remark}[Exact mixed reduction in terms of $\mathcal Z$ and $\mathcal R$]

\begin{proposition}[Interval-persistent nonmixed $4$-point degeneracy forces affine planarity]
\label{prop:interval-persistent-nonmixed-four-point-forces-affine-planarity}
Let \(J\) be a connected open interval in a good \(v_5\)-patch such that
\[
K_v\neq 0
\qquad\text{on }J.
\]
Assume
\[
T_v\equiv 0
\qquad\text{on }J.
\]
Then on \(J\) one has
\[
\mathcal R\equiv 0,
\qquad
\mathcal Z\equiv \gamma
\]
for some constant \(\gamma\in\mathbf C\), and hence
\[
Y=\gamma S+ax+b
\]
for some constants \(a,b\in\mathbf C\). Equivalently, the lifted curve
\[
x\longmapsto (Y(x),x,S(x))
\]
lies in an affine plane on \(J\).

In particular, any interval-persistent nonmixed exact lifted \(4\)-point branch
collapses to an affine-planar residual rather than remaining genuinely
three-dimensional.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
On the nonmixed interval \(J\), Proposition~\ref{prop:canonical-Z-R-invariants-lifted-four-point}
gives
\[
\mathcal R=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}.
\]
Since \(T_v\equiv 0\) and \(K_v\neq 0\) on \(J\), it follows that
\[
\mathcal R\equiv 0
\qquad\text{on }J.
\]
By definition,
\[
\mathcal R=\frac{d\mathcal Z}{dx},
\]
so \(\mathcal Z\) is constant on \(J\):
\[
\mathcal Z\equiv \gamma.
\]
Using
\[
\mathcal Z=\frac{Y''}{S''},
\]
we obtain
\[
Y''=\gamma S''.
\]
Integrating twice with respect to \(x\) yields
\[
Y=\gamma S+ax+b
\]
for constants \(a,b\in\mathbf C\). Therefore the lifted curve satisfies the
affine linear equation
\[
Y-\gamma S-ax-b=0
\]
throughout \(J\), so it lies in an affine plane.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Derivative-geometry meaning of the nonmixed invariant \(\mathcal R\)]
\label{rem:derivative-geometry-meaning-of-R}
On a nonmixed patch,
\[
\mathcal R=\frac{T_v}{K_v^2}
=
\frac{d}{dx}\!\left(\frac{Y''}{S''}\right).
\]
Thus \(\mathcal R\) measures the failure of the lifted space curve
\[
x\longmapsto (Y(x),x,S(x))
\]
to be affine-planar at the derivative level: \(\mathcal R=0\) means the ratio
\(Y''/S''\) is locally constant, equivalently that the curve has vanishing
affine torsion and lies in an affine plane after integrating twice. In this
sense, the nonmixed \(4\)-point branch is controlled by a single scalar
derivative invariant rather than by raw coplanarity alone.
\end{remark}

The second is that the exact NN-point kernel has a very clean positive, strictly decreasing real profile, and in fact the boundary sup on a circle is attained at the positive real point. That should help the effective-threshold side. This matches and sharpens the Connes/Mellin discussion already in the draft.

3. What these two pushes buy you

The smoothing lemma gives you a genuine smoothed Mellin projector theorem:

    the low odd-jet cancellation is still exact after smoothing;

    the smoothed projector differs from the discrete one only by O(Qη2)O(Qη2) on bounded second derivatives;

    this is the cleanest current route from the Connes/Mellin discussion to an honest test-function layer.

The kernel theorem gives you a genuine effective boundary diagnostic:

    the real boundary profile is positive,

    strictly decreasing in the boundary radius,

    and controls the whole boundary exactly.

That means the most pessimistic boundary point in the zeta-side contour estimate is the smallest admissible positive radius. In particular, if you later need explicit constants, you no longer need a vague “uniform on the boundary circle” estimate for the kernel itself — it is explicit and monotone.

If that works, you can recast the endgame in a smoother test-function language without losing the exact cancellation.

\begin{remark}[Sharper prefactor in the optimized zeta-side bound]
\label{rem:optimized-zeta-prefactor}

\begin{proposition}[Real profile of the exact \(N\)-point kernel]
\label{prop:exact-kernel-real-profile}
Let
\[
C_N:=(2N-1)N!(N-1)!,
\qquad
\kappa_N(x):=
x\,\frac{\Gamma(x-N)}{\Gamma(x+N+1)}
\qquad (x>N).
\]
Then
\[
\kappa_N(x)=\frac{1}{\prod_{j=1}^N(x^2-j^2)},
\]
and for real \(x>N\),
\[
K_{N,Q}\!\left(\frac{x}{Q^2}\right)
=
2(-1)^{N+1}C_NQ^2\,\kappa_N(x).
\]
Moreover:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\kappa_N(x)>0\) for all \(x>N\);
\item \(\kappa_N'(x)<0\) for all \(x>N\);
\item for every \(x>N\),
\[
\sup_{|\xi|=x/Q^2}|K_{N,Q}(\xi)|
=
2C_NQ^2\,\kappa_N(x)
=
\left|K_{N,Q}\!\left(\frac{x}{Q^2}\right)\right|.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
From Proposition~\ref{prop:corrected-n-point},
\[
K_{N,Q}(\xi)
=
2(-1)^{N+1}C_NQ^4\xi\,
\frac{\Gamma(Q^2\xi-N)}{\Gamma(Q^2\xi+N+1)}.
\]
Substituting \(\xi=x/Q^2\) gives
\[
K_{N,Q}\!\left(\frac{x}{Q^2}\right)
=
2(-1)^{N+1}C_NQ^2\,
x\,\frac{\Gamma(x-N)}{\Gamma(x+N+1)}
=
2(-1)^{N+1}C_NQ^2\,\kappa_N(x).
\]

Now
\[
\Gamma(x+N+1)
=
(x+N)(x+N-1)\cdots x\,\Gamma(x),
\]
and
\[
\Gamma(x)
=
(x-1)(x-2)\cdots (x-N)\,\Gamma(x-N).
\]
Hence
\[
x\,\frac{\Gamma(x-N)}{\Gamma(x+N+1)}
=
\frac{1}{(x-N)\cdots(x-1)(x+1)\cdots(x+N)}
=
\frac{1}{\prod_{j=1}^N(x^2-j^2)},
\]
which proves the product formula. Positivity is immediate for \(x>N\).

For monotonicity, differentiate the logarithm:
\[
\frac{d}{dx}\log\kappa_N(x)
=
-\sum_{j=1}^N \frac{2x}{x^2-j^2}.
\]
Since \(x>N\), each denominator is positive, so
\[
\frac{d}{dx}\log\kappa_N(x)<0,
\]
hence \(\kappa_N'(x)<0\).

Finally, using the product form of the kernel,
\[
K_{N,Q}(\xi)
=
2(-1)^{N+1}C_NQ^2\,
\frac{1}{\prod_{j=1}^N(Q^4\xi^2-j^2)},
\]
if \(|\xi|=x/Q^2\) then
\[
|Q^4\xi^2-j^2|
\ge \bigl||Q^2\xi|^2-j^2\bigr|
=
x^2-j^2
\qquad (1\le j\le N),
\]
so
\[
|K_{N,Q}(\xi)|
\le
2C_NQ^2\,
\frac{1}{\prod_{j=1}^N(x^2-j^2)}
=
2C_NQ^2\,\kappa_N(x).
\]
Equality is attained at \(\xi=x/Q^2\), proving the final claim.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[No contour loss in the kernel factor]
\label{rem:no-contour-loss-kernel-factor}
Proposition~\ref{prop:exact-kernel-real-profile} shows that the kernel factor
in Proposition~\ref{prop:corrected-n-point} is controlled exactly by its
positive real value on the boundary circle.  Thus the boundary estimate used in
the zeta-side \(N\)-point bound carries no hidden contour-loss from the kernel
itself: the worst point is the positive real boundary point
\[
\xi=\frac{\rho_0}{Q}.
\]
Moreover, since \(\kappa_N\) is strictly decreasing on \((N,\infty)\), the
kernel factor is largest at the smallest admissible boundary radius.
\end{remark}

2. Insert immediately after

\end{remark}

for

\begin{remark}[Discrete Mellin projector and test-function viewpoint]
\label{rem:discrete-mellin-projector}

\begin{lemma}[Smoothed Mellin projector]
\label{lem:smoothed-mellin-projector}
Let \(\phi\in C_c^\infty(\mathbf R)\) be even and normalized by
\[
\int_{\mathbf R}\phi(u)\,du=1.
\]
Define its bilateral Laplace transform by
\[
\widehat\phi(w):=\int_{\mathbf R}\phi(u)e^{wu}\,du.
\]
For \(\eta>0\), define the smoothed additive projector
\[
(\mathcal P_{N,Q,\eta}^{\phi}F)
:=
\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}
\int_{\mathbf R}\phi(u)\,
F\!\left(\frac{j}{Q^2}+\eta u\right)\,du,
\]
whenever \(F\) is holomorphic on a neighborhood of the integration region.

Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item for every exponential mode \(F(s)=e^{zs}\),
\[
\mathcal P_{N,Q,\eta}^{\phi}(e^{zs})
=
\widehat\phi(\eta z)\,\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(z),
\qquad
\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(z)=\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}e^{zj/Q^2};
\]

\item the smoothed symbol preserves the exact low odd-jet annihilation:
\[
\widehat\phi(0)\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(0)=1,
\qquad
\partial_z^{\,2r+1}\!\bigl(\widehat\phi(\eta z)\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(z)\bigr)\Big|_{z=0}=0
\quad (0\le r\le N-2);
\]

\item if \(F\) is holomorphic on a neighborhood \(\Omega\) containing all points
\[
\frac{j}{Q^2}+\eta u
\qquad
(1\le j\le N,\ u\in \operatorname{supp}\phi),
\]
then
\[
\bigl|\mathcal P_{N,Q,\eta}^{\phi}F-\mathcal P_{N,Q}F\bigr|
\le
\frac{\eta^2}{2}\,(2N-1)\,m_2(\phi)\,
\sup_{\Omega}|F''|,
\]
where
\[
m_2(\phi):=\int_{\mathbf R}|u|^2\,|\phi(u)|\,du.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
For \(F(s)=e^{zs}\),
\[
\mathcal P_{N,Q,\eta}^{\phi}(e^{zs})
=
\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}e^{zj/Q^2}
\int_{\mathbf R}\phi(u)e^{\eta zu}\,du
=
\widehat\phi(\eta z)\,\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(z),
\]
which proves the first statement.

Since \(\phi\) is even, \(\widehat\phi\) is an even entire function. In
particular,
\[
\widehat\phi(0)=\int_{\mathbf R}\phi(u)\,du=1,
\qquad
\widehat\phi^{(2r+1)}(0)=0
\quad (r\ge 0).
\]
Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-mellin-symbol} gives
\[
\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(0)=1,
\qquad
\partial_z^{\,2r+1}\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(0)=0
\quad (0\le r\le N-2).
\]
Applying Leibniz’ rule to the product
\(\widehat\phi(\eta z)\widehat\mu_{N,Q}(z)\) shows that all odd derivatives up
to order \(2N-3\) vanish at \(z=0\), proving the second statement.

For the quantitative comparison, expand \(F\) at \(j/Q^2\):
\[
F\!\left(\frac{j}{Q^2}+\eta u\right)
=
F\!\left(\frac{j}{Q^2}\right)
+
\eta u\,F'\!\left(\frac{j}{Q^2}\right)
+
\frac{\eta^2u^2}{2}\,F''(\xi_{j,u})
\]
for some \(\xi_{j,u}\in\Omega\). Since \(\phi\) is even,
\[
\int_{\mathbf R}\phi(u)\,u\,du=0.
\]
Also \(\int \phi(u)\,du=1\). Therefore
\[
\int_{\mathbf R}\phi(u)\,
F\!\left(\frac{j}{Q^2}+\eta u\right)\,du
-
F\!\left(\frac{j}{Q^2}\right)
=
\frac{\eta^2}{2}
\int_{\mathbf R}\phi(u)\,u^2\,F''(\xi_{j,u})\,du.
\]
Summing against \(a_j^{(N)}\) and using
Lemma~\ref{lem:n-point-coefficients},
\[
\sum_{j=1}^N |a_j^{(N)}|=2N-1,
\]
gives
\[
\bigl|\mathcal P_{N,Q,\eta}^{\phi}F-\mathcal P_{N,Q}F\bigr|
\le
\frac{\eta^2}{2}\,(2N-1)\,m_2(\phi)\,
\sup_{\Omega}|F''|.
\qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Smoothing scale]
\label{rem:smoothing-scale-mellin-projector}
Since \(N\asymp Q\), Lemma~\ref{lem:smoothed-mellin-projector} gives
\[
\mathcal P_{N,Q,\eta}^{\phi}F
=
\mathcal P_{N,Q}F
+
O\!\bigl(Q\eta^2\sup|F''|\bigr).
\]
Thus any choice \(\eta=o(Q^{-1/2})\) makes the smoothing error \(o(1)\) while
preserving the exact low odd-jet annihilation. This does not enter the present
proof, but it shows that the discrete \(N\)-point projector admits a genuinely
smooth Mellin-side avatar with the same exact cancellation pattern.
\end{remark}

These are the two pushes I would preserve now.

What they buy you is clean.

The smoothing lemma upgrades the Connes/Mellin discussion from orientation to a real operator theorem:
you now have a smooth multiplicative test function whose symbol still kills the first N−1N−1 odd jets exactly, and whose difference from the discrete projector is quantitatively controlled.

The kernel proposition gives you a real effective-theory gain:
the kernel profile is positive, strictly decreasing, and the boundary sup is exactly the positive real value. So the kernel factor in the zeta-side contour estimate is now fully canonical and has no hidden contour-loss.

\begin{remark}[Smoothing scale]
\label{rem:smoothing-scale-mellin-projector}
...
\end{remark}

This packages the smoothed projector into a genuine zeta-side theorem.

\begin{proposition}[Smoothed comparison for the corrected \(N\)-point zeta scalar]
\label{prop:smoothed-zeta-n-point-comparison}
Let \(\phi\in C_c^\infty(\mathbf R)\) be even, normalized by
\[
\int_{\mathbf R}\phi(u)\,du=1,
\]
and set
\[
U_\phi:=\sup\{|u|:u\in \operatorname{supp}\phi\},
\qquad
m_2(\phi):=\int_{\mathbf R}|u|^2\,|\phi(u)|\,du.
\]
Fix \(0<\alpha<\rho_0\), let
\[
N=\lfloor \alpha Q\rfloor,
\]
and assume
\[
0<\eta\le \frac{\rho_0-\alpha}{2U_\phi\,Q}.
\]
Define the smoothed corrected scalar
\[
H_{m,\eta}(s):=\int_{\mathbf R}\phi(u)\,H_m(s+\eta u)\,du,
\]
and the corresponding smoothed \(N\)-point scalar
\[
\Xi_{\zeta,\eta}^{(N)}(m)
:=
\sum_{j=1}^N a_j^{(N)}\,H_{m,\eta}\!\left(\frac{j}{Q^2}\right).
\]
Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(H_{m,\eta}\) is odd and holomorphic on
\[
|s|<\frac{\rho_\eta}{Q},
\qquad
\rho_\eta:=\rho_0-U_\phi \eta Q>\alpha.
\]

\item One has the zeta-side exponential estimate
\[
|\Xi_{\zeta,\eta}^{(N)}(m)|
\ll_{\phi,\rho_0,\alpha}
\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q}\,
e^{-\delta_*(\alpha,\rho_\eta)Q},
\]
where
\[
\delta_*(\alpha,\rho_\eta)
=
\rho_\eta\Bigl(
(1+\beta_\eta)\log(1+\beta_\eta)
-2\beta_\eta\log\beta_\eta
-(1-\beta_\eta)\log(1-\beta_\eta)
\Bigr),
\qquad
\beta_\eta:=\frac{\alpha}{\rho_\eta}.
\]

\item The smoothed and discrete \(N\)-point scalars satisfy
\[
|\Xi_{\zeta,\eta}^{(N)}(m)-\Xi_{\zeta}^{(N)}(m)|
\le
\frac{4\,C_{\partial}(\rho_0)\,m_2(\phi)}{(\rho_0-\alpha)^2}\,
\frac{2N-1}{Q}\,
\eta^2\,L(m)S(m)^2.
\]
In particular,
\[
|\Xi_{\zeta,\eta}^{(N)}(m)-\Xi_{\zeta}^{(N)}(m)|
\ll_{\phi,\rho_0,\alpha}
\eta^2\,L(m)S(m)^2.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Since \(H_m\) is odd and \(\phi\) is even,
\[
H_{m,\eta}(-s)
=
\int_{\mathbf R}\phi(u)\,H_m(-s+\eta u)\,du
=
\int_{\mathbf R}\phi(u)\,H_m(-(s+\eta u))\,du
=
-H_{m,\eta}(s),
\]
so \(H_{m,\eta}\) is odd. If \(|s|<\rho_\eta/Q\) and \(u\in \operatorname{supp}\phi\),
then
\[
|s+\eta u|
\le
|s|+\eta U_\phi
<
\frac{\rho_0-U_\phi\eta Q}{Q}+\eta U_\phi
=
\frac{\rho_0}{Q}.
\]
Since \(H_m\) is holomorphic on \(|s|<\rho_0/Q\), it follows that
\(H_{m,\eta}\) is holomorphic on \(|s|<\rho_\eta/Q\). This proves (1).

For \(|s|=\rho_\eta/Q\) and \(u\in \operatorname{supp}\phi\), the same estimate gives
\[
|s+\eta u|\le \frac{\rho_0}{Q}.
\]
Hence Proposition~\ref{prop:boundary-estimate} implies
\[
|H_m(s+\eta u)|
\le
C_{\partial}(\rho_0)\,\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q^3}.
\]
Therefore
\[
|H_{m,\eta}(s)|
\le
\|\phi\|_{L^1}\,C_{\partial}(\rho_0)\,\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q^3}
\qquad
\left(|s|=\frac{\rho_\eta}{Q}\right).
\]
Since \(H_{m,\eta}\) is odd and holomorphic on \(|s|<\rho_\eta/Q\), the proof of
Corollary~\ref{cor:optimized-zeta-cancellation-depth} applies verbatim with
\(\rho_0\) replaced by \(\rho_\eta\). This gives
\[
|\Xi_{\zeta,\eta}^{(N)}(m)|
\ll_{\phi,\rho_0,\alpha}
\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q}\,
e^{-\delta_*(\alpha,\rho_\eta)Q},
\]
proving (2).

For (3), note that
\[
\Xi_{\zeta,\eta}^{(N)}(m)-\Xi_{\zeta}^{(N)}(m)
=
\bigl(\mathcal P_{N,Q,\eta}^{\phi}-\mathcal P_{N,Q}\bigr)H_m.
\]
Apply Lemma~\ref{lem:smoothed-mellin-projector} with \(F=H_m\). It remains to
bound \(H_m''\) on the relevant region. Since
\[
\frac{j}{Q^2}\le \frac{N}{Q^2}\le \frac{\alpha}{Q},
\qquad
\eta U_\phi\le \frac{\rho_0-\alpha}{2Q},
\]
all points
\[
\frac{j}{Q^2}+\eta u
\qquad
(1\le j\le N,\ u\in\operatorname{supp}\phi)
\]
lie in the disk
\[
|s|\le \frac{\alpha}{Q}+\eta U_\phi
\le
\frac{\rho_0+\alpha}{2Q}.
\]
Thus their distance to the boundary circle \(|s|=\rho_0/Q\) is at least
\[
\frac{\rho_0-\alpha}{2Q}.
\]
By Cauchy’s derivative estimate and Proposition~\ref{prop:boundary-estimate},
\[
\sup_{\Omega}|H_m''|
\le
\frac{2\,C_{\partial}(\rho_0)\,L(m)S(m)^2/Q^3}
{\bigl((\rho_0-\alpha)/(2Q)\bigr)^2}
=
\frac{8\,C_{\partial}(\rho_0)}{(\rho_0-\alpha)^2}\,
\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q}.
\]
Lemma~\ref{lem:smoothed-mellin-projector} therefore yields
\[
|\Xi_{\zeta,\eta}^{(N)}(m)-\Xi_{\zeta}^{(N)}(m)|
\le
\frac{\eta^2}{2}\,(2N-1)\,m_2(\phi)\,
\frac{8\,C_{\partial}(\rho_0)}{(\rho_0-\alpha)^2}\,
\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q},
\]
which is exactly the stated bound.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Microscopic smoothing regime]
\label{rem:microscopic-smoothing-zeta-n-point}
If
\[
\eta\ll Q^{-2},
\]
then Proposition~\ref{prop:smoothed-zeta-n-point-comparison} gives
\[
\Xi_{\zeta,\eta}^{(N)}(m)
=
\Xi_{\zeta}^{(N)}(m)
+
O\!\left(\frac{L(m)S(m)^2}{Q^4}\right).
\]
At the same time,
\[
\rho_\eta=\rho_0+O(Q^{-1}),
\]
so
\[
\delta_*(\alpha,\rho_\eta)
=
\delta_*(\alpha,\rho_0)+O(Q^{-1}).
\]
Thus microscopic smoothing preserves the same zeta-side exponential scale while
replacing the discrete projector by a genuinely smooth Mellin-side test
function.
\end{remark}

This gives you exactly the hybrid statement you wanted:

    the smoothed NN-point zeta scalar obeys the same exponential estimate as the discrete one, with the obvious ρηρη​-adjustment;

    the difference between smoothed and discrete is controlled by an explicit smoothing error term.

The key practical takeaway is the last remark: on the natural microscopic scale
η≍Q−2,
η≍Q−2,

the smoothing error is tiny, while the exponential zeta-side bound stays on the same scale. That is probably the cleanest way to preserve the Connes/Mellin discussion as something mathematically real rather than purely philosophical.

anything else to gain on this line?

Yes — but the returns are starting to diminish, and the remaining gains on this line are mostly about effectivity, canonicity, and smoother packaging, not the finite-core bottleneck.

The best further gains I see are these.

First, you can upgrade the smoothing lemma to higher-order smoothing. Right now the comparison error is quadratic:
PN,Q,ηϕF−PN,QF=O ⁣(Qη2sup⁡∣F′′∣).
PN,Q,ηϕ​F−PN,Q​F=O(Qη2sup∣F′′∣).

If you allow a sign-changing even test function ϕϕ with moment conditions
∫ϕ(u) du=1,∫u2kϕ(u) du=0(1≤k≤M−1),
∫ϕ(u)du=1,∫u2kϕ(u)du=0(1≤k≤M−1),

then the same argument gives
PN,Q,ηϕF−PN,QF=O ⁣(Qη2Msup⁡∣F(2M)∣).
PN,Q,ηϕ​F−PN,Q​F=O(Qη2Msup∣F(2M)∣).

That is a real theorem-level strengthening. It means you can make the smoothed projector arbitrarily close to the discrete one while still preserving the exact low odd-jet annihilation, because ϕ^ϕ
​ remains even. This is probably the single best remaining gain on this Mellin/test-function line.

Second, you can prove a smoothed kernel profile theorem. For nonnegative even ϕϕ, define the smoothed real profile by averaging the exact real kernel:
κN,ηϕ(x):=∫ϕ(u) κN(x+ηQ2u) du
κN,ηϕ​(x):=∫ϕ(u)κN​(x+ηQ2u)du

whenever x+ηQ2u>Nx+ηQ2u>N on the support of ϕϕ. Since κNκN​ is positive and strictly decreasing, the averaged profile is still positive and decreasing. So the smoothed projector inherits the same qualitative real-boundary behavior as the discrete kernel. That would give you a nice companion theorem to the exact kernel-profile proposition.

Third, you can probably extract a canonical optimal smoothing scale by balancing two effects:

    the comparison error
    O(Qη2)or more generallyO(Qη2M),
    O(Qη2)or more generallyO(Qη2M),

    and the radius loss
    ρη=ρ0−UϕηQ,
    ρη​=ρ0​−Uϕ​ηQ,

    which perturbs the exponent
    δ∗(α,ρη).
    δ∗​(α,ρη​).

On the current quadratic smoothing, η≍Q−2η≍Q−2 is already the natural microscopic scale. With higher-order moment cancellation, you may be able to push ηη much larger while keeping the comparison error negligible. That would make the “smooth test-function avatar” feel less artificially microscopic.

Fourth, there is a plausible Toeplitz/Hankel positivity package for the smoothed symbol
f^N,Q,η(z)=ϕ^(ηz)μ^N,Q(z).
f
​N,Q,η​(z)=ϕ
​(ηz)μ
​N,Q​(z).

This is the most Connes-flavored remaining gain. Because the symbol is explicit, has exact low odd-jet vanishing, and can be chosen smooth, you can form moment matrices from its Taylor data or boundary values and ask whether they satisfy positivity / rank / variation-diminishing properties. I would treat this as exploratory rather than a near-term theorem, but it is probably the most natural place to push if you want a stronger operator-theoretic wrapper.

Fifth, and most practical, you can try to turn the current kernel/smoothing package into an explicit-threshold endgame lemma. You now have:

    exact kernel profile,

    explicit optimized exponent δ∗(α,ρ0)δ∗​(α,ρ0​),

    explicit prefactor asymptotics,

    and a controlled smoothed comparison.

So the remaining hidden constant on this line is basically the boundary constant C∂(ρ0)C∂​(ρ0​). Once that is explicit, this whole Mellin/kernel apparatus should produce a concrete zeta-side constant. That is probably the most useful downstream gain for the paper as a proof architecture.

So my ranking would be:

    Higher-order smoothing by moment-corrected ϕϕ — best theorem-level gain still available here.

    Smoothed kernel positivity/monotonicity — nice companion result, low risk.

    Optimal smoothing-scale analysis — helpful for making the package look canonical.

    Explicit-threshold extraction — best practical payoff if you can expose C∂(ρ0)C∂​(ρ0​).

    Toeplitz/Hankel positivity package — interesting, but more speculative.

If you want to keep pushing this line right now, I would do the first one next: prove the higher-order smoothing lemma. That is the cleanest remaining mathematical upgrade.

I’m pushing the smoothing line one step further: the clean upgrade is to replace “quadratic smoothing error” by an arbitrary even-order moment-corrected smoothing scheme and see how that propagates into the zeta-side estimate.

I pushed the higher-order smoothing line. The good news is that it works cleanly. The bad news is that, under the existing admissibility ceiling η=O(1/Q)η=O(1/Q), the asymptotic gain saturates at order Q−2Q−2 unless you deliberately choose a smaller smoothing scale.

Here is the next clean theorem on this line.

Let M≥1M≥1, and choose an even compactly supported smooth kernel ϕM∈Cc∞(R)ϕM​∈Cc∞​(R) such that
∫RϕM(u) du=1,∫Ru2ℓϕM(u) du=0(1≤ℓ≤M−1).
∫R​ϕM​(u)du=1,∫R​u2ℓϕM​(u)du=0(1≤ℓ≤M−1).

Because ϕMϕM​ is even, all odd moments vanish automatically. Its bilateral Laplace transform
ϕ^M(w):=∫RϕM(u)ewu du
ϕ
​M​(w):=∫R​ϕM​(u)ewudu

then satisfies
ϕ^M(0)=1,ϕ^M(k)(0)=0(1≤k≤2M−1).
ϕ
​M​(0)=1,ϕ
​M(k)​(0)=0(1≤k≤2M−1).

So
ϕ^M(ηz)=1+O((ηz)2M).
ϕ
​M​(ηz)=1+O((ηz)2M).

Now define the 2M2M-th order smoothed additive projector
(PN,Q,η(M)F):=∑j=1Naj(N)∫RϕM(u) F ⁣(jQ2+ηu) du.
(PN,Q,η(M)​F):=j=1∑N​aj(N)​∫R​ϕM​(u)F(Q2j​+ηu)du.

Then the exact analogue of the previous smoothing lemma is:
PN,Q,η(M)(ezs)=ϕ^M(ηz) μ^N,Q(z).
PN,Q,η(M)​(ezs)=ϕ
​M​(ηz)μ
​N,Q​(z).​

Since ϕ^Mϕ

∂z2r+1 ⁣(ϕ^M(ηz)μ^N,Q(z))∣z=0=0(0≤r≤N−2).
∂z2r+1​(ϕ
​M​(ηz)μ
​N,Q​(z))
​z=0​=0(0≤r≤N−2).

And the comparison error improves to order 2M2M:
∣PN,Q,η(M)F−PN,QF∣≤η2M(2M)! (2N−1) m2M(ϕM) sup⁡Ω∣F(2M)∣,
​PN,Q,η(M)​F−PN,Q​F
​≤(2M)!η2M​(2N−1)m2M​(ϕM​)Ωsup​∣F(2M)∣,​

where
m2M(ϕM):=∫R∣u∣2M∣ϕM(u)∣ du.
m2M​(ϕM​):=∫R​∣u∣2M∣ϕM​(u)∣du.

That is the abstract higher-order smoothing theorem.

Now apply it to the corrected zeta scalar HmHm​. The same Cauchy argument as before gives, on the admissible region,
sup⁡Ω∣Hm(2M)∣≤(2M)! C∂(ρ0)(2ρ0−α)2MQ2M−3 L(m)S(m)2.
Ωsup​∣Hm(2M)​∣≤(2M)!C∂​(ρ0​)(ρ0​−α2​)2MQ2M−3L(m)S(m)2.

Substituting this into the projector bound yields
∣Ξζ,η(N,M)(m)−Ξζ(N)(m)∣≤C∂(ρ0) (2N−1) m2M(ϕM)(2ρ0−α)2Mη2MQ2M−3 L(m)S(m)2,
∣Ξζ,η(N,M)​(m)−Ξζ(N)​(m)∣≤C∂​(ρ0​)(2N−1)m2M​(ϕM​)(ρ0​−α2​)2Mη2MQ2M−3L(m)S(m)2,​

or, since N≍QN≍Q,
∣Ξζ,η(N,M)(m)−Ξζ(N)(m)∣≪ϕM,ρ0,α(ηQ)2M L(m)S(m)2Q2.
∣Ξζ,η(N,M)​(m)−Ξζ(N)​(m)∣≪ϕM​,ρ0​,α​(ηQ)2MQ2L(m)S(m)2​.​

That is the key sharpened formula.

What this means in practice is slightly subtle.

If you stay at the largest admissible smoothing scale
η≍1Q,
η≍Q1​,

then
(ηQ)2M≍1,
(ηQ)2M≍1,

so the error is still only
O ⁣(L(m)S(m)2Q2).
O(Q2L(m)S(m)2​).

So under the current admissibility ceiling, higher-order smoothing does not improve the QQ-power in the zeta comparison. It improves the constant structure and the symbolic canonicity, but not the asymptotic order.

If instead you choose a genuinely smaller smoothing scale
η=Q−1−σ,σ>0,
η=Q−1−σ,σ>0,

then
(ηQ)2M=Q−2Mσ,
(ηQ)2M=Q−2Mσ,

and the error becomes
∣Ξζ,η(N,M)(m)−Ξζ(N)(m)∣≪Q−2−2Mσ L(m)S(m)2.
∣Ξζ,η(N,M)​(m)−Ξζ(N)​(m)∣≪Q−2−2MσL(m)S(m)2.​

So higher-order smoothing only gives a genuine asymptotic gain when you deliberately work below the natural 1/Q1/Q scale.

That is the honest verdict.

So what do we actually gain?

Three things.

First, we now have a fully general smooth Mellin-projector family:
for every MM, there exists a smoothed projector that preserves the exact low odd-jet annihilation and differs from the discrete one only at order 2M2M.

Second, this makes the Connes/Mellin language much more solid: the discrete projector is not just approximable by a smooth test function, but by an arbitrarily high-order moment-corrected smooth test function with the same exact odd-jet behavior near z=0z=0.

Third, it clarifies the limit of this line: unless you can relax the admissible smoothing radius beyond O(1/Q)O(1/Q), higher-order smoothing will not dramatically improve the quantitative endgame by itself. Its main value is canonicity and smooth packaging, not closing the remaining RH bottleneck.

There is one more modest gain on this line that is still available: if you take ϕM≥0ϕM​≥0, then the smoothed real kernel profile
κN,η(M)(x):=∫ϕM(u) κN(x+ηQ2u) du
κN,η(M)​(x):=∫ϕM​(u)κN​(x+ηQ2u)du

inherits positivity and monotonic decrease from κNκN​, as long as x+ηQ2u>Nx+ηQ2u>N on the support. So even after smoothing, the kernel side remains well-behaved on the real axis.

My honest recommendation is: this higher-order smoothing theorem is worth preserving in the paper only if you want the Mellin/test-function line to look like a real operator package rather than a side remark. It is a clean structural result. But it is probably not where the proof will live or die.
